  manifold.  Mathlib has \code{TangentBundle}; the alternating-form
  package on \(T^{*}M\) is missing.
\item \textbf{R9-sub-B.}  Smooth-section infrastructure for a vector
  bundle.  \code{Mathlib.Geometry.Manifold.VectorBundle.SmoothSection}
  exists; the alternating-form variant is not yet packaged.
\item \textbf{R9-sub-C.}  Chart-local description: in a chart,
  \(\Omega^{k}(M)\) restricts to alternating \(k\)-multilinear maps on
  \(\R^{n}\), with \(d\) given by partial derivatives.
\end{itemize}

\begin{theorem}[Classical input: bundled differential forms $\Omega^{k}(M)$]
\label{input:bundled-omega-k}
\uses{lem:bundled-forms-algebra,lem:bundled-forms-operations}
The Lean target
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.BundledForms.bundled\_forms\_overview} states
the existence of an \(\R\)-vector space \(\Omega^{k}(M)\) with the
operations \(d\), \(\wedge\), pullback, and integration, all
simultaneously linear and natural, with sub-leaves R9.1.1–R9.6.2
tracked in \code{Jacobian/Analysis/BundledForms/Overview.lean}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.BundledForms.bundled_forms_overview}
\leanok
\end{theorem}


\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Three rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly
lemma above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean
declaration (or a Mathlib reference) and is marked
\textsf{leanok} when the Lean stub exists, \textsf{notready}
when the statement is still a forward declaration, or
\textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Bundled forms step 1: $\Omega^{k}(M)$ algebra]
\label{lem:bundled-forms-algebra}
\uses{lem:bfa-r1}
$\Omega^{k}(M)$ is an $\R$-vector space and a $C^{\infty}(M, \R)$-module.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.BundledForms.bundled_forms_algebra}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Bundled forms step 2: $d, \wedge$ \(\R\)-linear with the standard identities]
\label{lem:bundled-forms-operations}
\uses{lem:bundled-forms-d-sq-zero,lem:bundled-forms-pullback-compat}
The exterior derivative $d : \Omega^{k} \to \Omega^{k+1}$ and wedge product $\wedge : \Omega^{p} \otimes \Omega^{q} \to \Omega^{p+q}$ exist as $\R$-linear / bilinear maps with $d^{2} = 0$, graded commutativity, and the Leibniz rule.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.BundledForms.bundled_forms_operations}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[$d^{2} = 0$]
\label{lem:bundled-forms-d-sq-zero}
\uses{lem:bfd-r1}
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.BundledForms.bundled_forms_d_sq_zero}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pullback by smooth maps commutes with $d$]
\label{lem:bundled-forms-pullback-compat}
\uses{lem:bfp-r1}
$f^{*} \circ d = d \circ f^{*}$ for every smooth map $f : M \to N$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.BundledForms.bundled_forms_pullback_compat}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
The simplest object in differential geometry is the smooth function
on a manifold.  One step up: a smooth 1-form, which at each point
pairs against tangent vectors to give a number.  Higher \(k\) packs
alternating-multilinear data.  The whole tower
\(\Omega^{0} \to \Omega^{1} \to \Omega^{2} \to \cdots\) carries the
exterior derivative \(d\), the wedge product \(\wedge\), pullback by
smooth maps, and (in top degree on a compact oriented manifold)
integration.  These four operations interact via the standard
identities \(d^{2}=0\), Leibniz, naturality, and Stokes that make
differential forms the universal language of integration on
manifolds.

Mathlib has the underlying machinery — manifold derivative,
alternating multilinear maps, partition-of-unity — but not the
integrated bundled-form package.  R9 is what you need to state
precisely ``the de Rham complex of \(M\),'' ``the exterior algebra
of \(M\),'' ``Stokes' formula on \(M\).''  Without R9, every theorem
about forms on a manifold has to talk around the missing object.
About 400–600 LOC for a complete API; \emph{foundational for R4, R5,
and R7 simultaneously}, which is why it gets its own node.
\end{layman}

%======================================================================
\subsection{R10 — Sobolev / elliptic regularity}
\label{subsec:gap-R10-sobolev-elliptic}

The headline says: for a compact oriented Riemannian manifold \(M\)
and \(k : \N\), the Sobolev space \(H^{s}(\Omega^{k}(M))\) is
well-defined for every \(s : \R\), and the Laplace–Beltrami operator
\(\Delta : H^{s+2}(\Omega^{k}) \to H^{s}(\Omega^{k})\) is
\emph{Fredholm}.  Furthermore every distributional solution of
\(\Delta\omega = f\) with smooth \(f\) is itself smooth (elliptic
regularity).

R10 is the \emph{single largest sub-gap} in the entire eight-node
tree — estimated 2500–4000 LOC.  It underpins R5 (Hodge decomposition)
and indirectly R7 (Dolbeault) and R8 (Serre duality via the
\(L^{2}\)-pairing realised by harmonic representatives).  Promoted
from ``R5-sub-B'' in the previous round to its own top-level
dep-graph node because it is both shared and substantial.

\subsubsection*{Classical content}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Sobolev framework.}  Define \(H^{s}(\Omega^{k}(M))\) for
  \(s : \R\) using a partition of unity and chart-local Sobolev norms
  on \(\R^{n}\).  Density of smooth forms; Sobolev embedding
  \(H^{s} \hookrightarrow C^{0}\) for \(s > n/2\).
\item \emph{Ellipticity of \(\Delta\).}  Principal symbol
  \(\sigma_{\Delta}(\xi) = |\xi|^{2} \cdot \mathrm{id}\), invertible
  for \(\xi \neq 0\).
\item \emph{Elliptic regularity.}  Garding inequality:
  \(\|\omega\|_{s+2} \leq C(\|\Delta\omega\|_{s} + \|\omega\|_{s})\).
  Smooth forms remain smooth after applying \(\Delta\).
\item \emph{Rellich--Kondrachov.}  On a compact manifold,
  \(H^{s'} \hookrightarrow H^{s}\) is compact for \(s' > s\).
\item \emph{Fredholm.}  Combining 3 + 4:
  \(\Delta : H^{s+2}(\Omega^{k}) \to H^{s}(\Omega^{k})\) is Fredholm.
  Hence the Hodge orthogonal decomposition
  \(\Omega^{k}(M) = \mathcal{H}^{k}(M) \oplus d(\Omega^{k-1}) \oplus
  \delta(\Omega^{k+1})\), with \(\mathcal{H}^{k}\) finite-dimensional.
\item \emph{Bochner / Kähler identities.}  On a Kähler manifold the
  Laplacians for \(d\), \(\partial\), \(\bar\partial\) are
  proportional, refining R10 into the R5 bigrading.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection*{Lean proof plan}

15 sub-leaves under
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.Overview}, in six phases.
Mathlib v4.28.0 has Sobolev spaces on \(\R^{n}\)
(\code{Mathlib.Analysis.Distribution.SchwartzSpace}) and the basic
Cauchy--Schwarz / Bessel apparatus, but \emph{no manifold-level
Sobolev theory} and \emph{no elliptic regularity for second-order
operators} on a manifold.  Building this from scratch is a major
undertaking — comparable in scope to formalising the basics of
Riemannian geometry.

\subsubsection*{Recursive sub-gaps surfaced}

\begin{itemize}
\item \textbf{R10-sub-A.}  Distribution theory on a smooth manifold
  (the foundation of Sobolev spaces).  ~600 LOC.
\item \textbf{R10-sub-B.}  The \(H^{s}\)-norm on \(\Omega^{k}(M)\):
  chart-local construction + partition of unity.  ~500 LOC.
\item \textbf{R10-sub-C.}  Garding inequality (Phase 3 prerequisite).
  ~300 LOC.
\item \textbf{R10-sub-D.}  Rellich--Kondrachov compactness theorem.
  ~400 LOC.
\item \textbf{R10-sub-E.}  Fredholm-alternative theorem on a compact
  manifold.  ~300 LOC.
\end{itemize}

\begin{theorem}[Classical input: Sobolev spaces and elliptic regularity for $\Delta$]
\label{input:sobolev-elliptic-regularity}
\uses{lem:sobolev-elliptic-kernel-finite-dim,lem:sobolev-fredholm-property}
The Lean target
\code{Jacobian.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.sobolev\_elliptic\_overview}
states the Fredholm property of the Laplacian on a compact Riemannian
manifold; sub-leaves R10.1.1–R10.6.1 are tracked in
\code{Jacobian/Analysis/SobolevElliptic/Overview.lean}.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.sobolev_elliptic_overview}
\leanok
\end{theorem}


\subsubsection*{Stepwise refinement of the proof}

Three rounds of top-down refinement decompose the assembly
lemma above into named sub-leaves.  Each leaf carries a Lean
declaration (or a Mathlib reference) and is marked
\textsf{leanok} when the Lean stub exists, \textsf{notready}
when the statement is still a forward declaration, or
\textsf{mathlibok} when it reduces to a Mathlib theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Sobolev step 1: ellipticity + finite-dim kernel]
\label{lem:sobolev-elliptic-kernel-finite-dim}
\uses{lem:sobolev-laplacian-elliptic,lem:sobolev-garding,lem:sobolev-rellich-kondrachov}
For a compact oriented Riemannian manifold, the Laplace–Beltrami operator $\Delta$ is elliptic and has finite-dimensional kernel.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.sobolev_elliptic_kernel_finite_dim}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[$\Delta$ is elliptic]
\label{lem:sobolev-laplacian-elliptic}
\uses{lem:sle-r1}
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.sobolev_laplacian_elliptic}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Garding inequality]
\label{lem:sobolev-garding}
\uses{lem:sgd-r1}
$\|\omega\|_{s+2} \leq C(\|T\omega\|_{s} + \|\omega\|_{s})$ for elliptic $T$.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.sobolev_garding}
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Rellich–Kondrachov compactness]
\label{lem:sobolev-rellich-kondrachov}
\uses{lem:rk-r1-partition-localize}
$H^{s'} \hookrightarrow H^{s}$ is compact for $s' > s$ on a compact manifold.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.sobolev_rellich_kondrachov}
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Sobolev step 2: Fredholm property of $\Delta$]
\label{lem:sobolev-fredholm-property}
\uses{lem:sobolev-elliptic-kernel-finite-dim,lem:sobolev-rellich-kondrachov}
From step 1 + Rellich–Kondrachov, $\Delta : H^{s+2}(\Omega^{k}) \to H^{s}(\Omega^{k})$ is Fredholm.
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Analysis.SobolevElliptic.sobolev_fredholm_property}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
When you ask ``does the equation \(\Delta\omega = f\) have a solution''
on a compact manifold, you face a problem: smooth forms are not
enough — the abstract solution operator (the inverse of \(\Delta\)
modulo its kernel) doesn't act on smooth forms in a closed way.  The
standard trick is to enlarge the space: replace \(\Omega^{k}(M)\) with
the Sobolev space \(H^{s}(\Omega^{k}(M))\), where \(s\) measures the
number of weak derivatives you allow.  In the larger space, \(\Delta :
H^{s+2} \to H^{s}\) becomes a Fredholm operator — its kernel and
cokernel are finite-dimensional, its range is closed — which is
exactly what you need to invert it on the orthogonal complement of the
kernel.

The deep result is \emph{elliptic regularity}: even though you allowed
yourself to enlarge the space, the actual solutions of \(\Delta\omega
= f\) for smooth \(f\) end up being smooth themselves.  So at the end
of the day you can think of \(\Delta\) as a Fredholm operator on
smooth forms, with a finite-dimensional kernel (the harmonic forms)
and a smooth inverse (the \emph{Green operator}) on the orthogonal
complement.  This is what powers Hodge decomposition (R5), and through
R5 the analytic-genus-equals-topological-genus identity (R6), and
through harmonic-form representatives the non-degeneracy of the Serre
pairing (R8).

Mathlib has Sobolev spaces on \(\R^{n}\) and Schwartz distributions,
but no manifold-level Sobolev theory and no elliptic regularity for
second-order operators on a manifold.  Building this from scratch is
the single largest budget item in the entire eight-gap tree —
estimated 2500–4000 LOC for the headline package, plus 2000–3000 LOC
for prerequisites.  It also has the deepest character: this is real
hard analysis, not algebraic topology bookkeeping.
\end{layman}

%======================================================================
\subsection{Summary of recursive sub-gaps}
\label{subsec:gap-summary}

The eight-node tree, when expanded one level into recursive sub-gaps,
surfaces a roughly 25-node graph.  Several sub-gaps are shared
across multiple parent nodes (the bundled \(\Omega^k\) package
appears under R4, R5, and R7; the Riemannian metric appears under R4
and R5; the abstract simplicial complex appears under R1 and R3).
A consolidated estimate of the total Lean-formalisation cost,
including all shared sub-gaps counted once, is:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{lr}
\textbf{Component} & \textbf{Estimated LOC} \\\hline
R1 + R1-sub-\{A,B,C\} & 2000--2700 \\
R2 + R2-sub-\{A,B,C\} & 900--1100 \\
R3 + R3-sub-\{A,B,C\} & 1500--2000 \\
R4 (modulo R9) & 1500--2000 \\
R5 (modulo R9, R10) + R5-sub-\{A,E\} & 1700--2500 \\
R6 + R6-sub-\{A,B,C\} & 1100--1400 \\
R7 (modulo R9, R10) + R7-sub-\{B,C,D\} & 1300--1700 \\
R8 + R8-sub-\{A,B,C,D\} & 3300--4300 \\
R9 + R9-sub-\{A,B,C\} & 800--1200 \\
R10 + R10-sub-\{A,B,C,D,E\} & 4500--6500 \\\hline
\textbf{Total (with shared deduplication)} & \textbf{18000--25000 LOC} \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

The dominant single component is R10 (Sobolev / elliptic regularity for
forms on a manifold), now its own dep-graph node, estimated at
2500--4000 LOC for the headline plus 2000--2500 LOC for sub-gaps.
R10 is on the critical path for R5 and (transitively) R7 + R8.

R9 (bundled \(\Omega^{k}\)) is the second largest piece of shared
infrastructure, on the critical path for R4, R5, and R7 simultaneously.

R6 remains the most \emph{blocked}: it depends on R4, R5, and R7
together, so it cannot land until all three are deep enough to expose
their output statements.  R3 remains the most \emph{ready}: its Lean
infrastructure is already largely in place.

\bigskip

\begin{layman}
Adding it all up: the eight major analysis gaps, expanded one level
into their internal sub-gaps, total around fifteen to twenty-two
thousand lines of Lean.  That's a substantial fraction of the entire
project but it's a knowable one — every node is named, every
dependency is identified, every sub-gap has at least a placeholder
Lean target.  The single biggest cost driver is \emph{Sobolev spaces
of forms on a manifold plus elliptic regularity} (R5-sub-B), which
underpins the Hodge decomposition (R5) and indirectly the Dolbeault
proof (R7).  Once that's in hand, much of the rest cascades.

The other large cost is \emph{bundled differential forms}
(R4-sub-A): a \(C^\infty(M)\)-module \(\Omega^k(M)\) with all the
expected operators (\(d\), \(\wedge\), pullback) simultaneously
linear, natural, and compatible.  Mathlib has the pieces
(\code{MFDeriv}, exterior algebra in finite dimensions, manifold
charts) but no integrated bundled form package; this is shared
infrastructure across R4, R5, and R7.

Among the eight, R6 is the smallest in itself but the most
\emph{blocked}: it depends on R4, R5, and R7 simultaneously, so it
cannot be touched until all three are deep enough to expose their
output statements.  R3, by contrast, is the most \emph{ready}: its
Lean infrastructure is already largely in place, blocked mainly on
R1 (Radó) and R2 (Tietze).  A pragmatic first move would be to
formalise R1 + R2 + R3 as a topology-only sub-project, in parallel
with R4-sub-A (bundled \(\Omega^k\)) and R5-sub-A (Riemannian
metric) as foundational analytic infrastructure.  Both of those
unblock the rest of the analytic chain.
\end{layman}

\subsection{Frontier gap analysis: 5-pass refinement of leaf nodes}
\label{subsec:frontier-five-pass}

The dependency graph still has periphery leaves whose proofs reduce
to classical analysis without an immediate Mathlib match.  This
subsection picks one leaf per major gap area (R1 Radó, R4 de Rham,
R7 Dolbeault, R8 Serre, R10 Sobolev) and refines it five levels
deep.  Each pass replaces the bare statement with a proof that
depends on a smaller sub-statement (still \texttt{\textbackslash notready}),
so the depth of the chain measures how far a frontier leaf is from
ordinary Mathlib.

In every chain below, pass~5 lands on a statement that either
\emph{is} in Mathlib already or is one named declaration away.

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain A: $\bar\partial$-Poincaré on a polydisk}

\begin{lemma}[Pass A.1: reduce to one-variable, $q=1$]
\label{lem:dpp-r1-onevar-suffices}
\uses{lem:dpp-r2-pompeiu-formula}
For an arbitrary polydisk $U = U_{1} \times \cdots \times U_{n}
\subseteq \C^{n}$, $\bar\partial$-exactness of every closed
$(p,q)$-form with $q \geq 1$ reduces to the one-variable case
$n=1, q=1$ by induction on $n$ and on $q$: write
$\omega = d\bar z_{n} \wedge \alpha + \beta$ where $\beta$ is free
of $d\bar z_{n}$, solve $\bar\partial_{z_{n}} u = \alpha$ in
$z_{n}$ alone (one-variable case), absorb $\bar\partial u$ into
$\omega$, and recurse.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass A.2: one-variable Pompeiu formula gives a primitive]
\label{lem:dpp-r2-pompeiu-formula}
\uses{lem:dpp-r3-stokes-on-disk}
For $f \in C^{\infty}_{c}(\C)$ supported in a disk $D_{R}$,
the Cauchy--Pompeiu transform
$Tf(z) := \frac{1}{2\pi i}\int_{\C} \frac{f(\zeta)}{\zeta - z}\,
d\zeta \wedge d\bar\zeta$
satisfies $\bar\partial_{z} Tf = f$ pointwise.  Hence on a disk,
every $\bar\partial$-closed $(0,1)$-form $f\,d\bar z$ with smooth
compactly supported coefficient is $\bar\partial$-exact via
$u = Tf$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass A.3: Stokes on a punctured disk yields the Pompeiu identity]
\label{lem:dpp-r3-stokes-on-disk}
\uses{lem:dpp-r4-kernel-locint}
The Pompeiu identity $f(z) = \frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_{\partial D}
\frac{f(\zeta)}{\zeta - z}\,d\zeta - \frac{1}{2\pi i}\iint_{D}
\frac{\bar\partial f(\zeta)}{\zeta - z}\,d\zeta\wedge d\bar\zeta$
follows from Stokes' theorem applied to $\frac{f(\zeta)}{\zeta - z}
d\zeta$ on $D \setminus \overline{B_{\varepsilon}(z)}$ and
letting $\varepsilon \to 0$, using local integrability of the
Cauchy kernel.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass A.4: the Cauchy kernel $1/(\zeta - z)$ is locally integrable on $\C$]
\label{lem:dpp-r4-kernel-locint}
\uses{lem:dpp-r5-polar-volume}
$\zeta \mapsto 1/|\zeta - z|$ is in $L^{1}_{\mathrm{loc}}(\C)$
for every $z$.  Concretely, for every $R > 0$,
$\iint_{B_{R}(z)} 1/|\zeta - z|\,dA(\zeta) = 2\pi R < \infty$.
\lean{MeasureTheory.integrable_one_div_nnorm}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass A.5: polar-coordinate area formula for $\int 1/r$ on a disk]
\label{lem:dpp-r5-polar-volume}
$\iint_{B_{R}(0)} \frac{1}{|w|}\,dA(w) =
\int_{0}^{2\pi}\int_{0}^{R} \frac{1}{r} \cdot r\,dr\,d\theta
= 2\pi R$.  Mathlib gap: change-of-variables to polar
coordinates on $\R^{2}$ for an $L^{1}_{\mathrm{loc}}$ function;
\code{MeasureTheory.integral\_comp\_polarCoord\_symm} is the
intended target.
\lean{MeasureTheory.integral_comp_polarCoord_symm}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B: Rellich--Kondrachov compactness}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B.1: localize via partition of unity on a compact manifold]
\label{lem:rk-r1-partition-localize}
\uses{lem:rk-r2-local-flat}
Compactness of $H^{s'}(M) \hookrightarrow H^{s}(M)$ on a compact
$M$ reduces to compactness of finitely many local embeddings
$H^{s'}(B^{n}_{1}) \hookrightarrow H^{s}(B^{n}_{1})$ via a smooth
finite partition of unity subordinate to a chart cover.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B.2: local case via Fourier extension to $\R^{n}$]
\label{lem:rk-r2-local-flat}
\uses{lem:rk-r3-fourier-characterization}
Every bounded family in $H^{s'}_{c}(B^{n}_{1})$ extends by
zero to a bounded family in $H^{s'}(\R^{n})$, so the local case
follows from compactness of the embedding $H^{s'}(\R^{n})
\cap \{\mathrm{supp} \subseteq K\} \hookrightarrow H^{s}(\R^{n})$
for $K$ compact.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B.3: Fourier characterization of $H^{s}(\R^{n})$]
\label{lem:rk-r3-fourier-characterization}
\uses{lem:rk-r4-truncation-compact}
$H^{s}(\R^{n}) = \{ u \in \mathcal{S}'(\R^{n}) :
(1+|\xi|^{2})^{s/2}\,\hat u \in L^{2}(\R^{n})\}$.  Compactness
on functions with support in $K$ then reduces to compactness of
the truncated embedding via Fourier multipliers.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B.4: high-frequency truncation gives compact embedding]
\label{lem:rk-r4-truncation-compact}
\uses{lem:rk-r5-tail-decay}
For $\chi_{N}$ the indicator of $\{|\xi| \leq N\}$,
$u \mapsto \mathcal{F}^{-1}(\chi_{N} \hat u)$ has finite-rank
range on a fixed compact-support family (Paley--Wiener), and the
remainder $u - \chi_{N} u$ has $H^{s}$-norm bounded by
$N^{-(s'-s)} \|u\|_{H^{s'}}$, which tends to $0$ as $N \to
\infty$.  Hence the embedding is approximated in operator norm
by finite-rank operators, making it compact.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B.5: tail decay in weighted $L^{2}$ is monotone convergence]
\label{lem:rk-r5-tail-decay}
$\int_{|\xi| > N} (1+|\xi|^{2})^{s} |\hat u(\xi)|^{2}\,d\xi
\to 0$ as $N \to \infty$ for $\hat u \in L^{2}(\R^{n};
(1+|\xi|^{2})^{s'} d\xi)$ and $s' > s$, by dominated
convergence on the integrand $(1+|\xi|^{2})^{s-s'} \cdot
(1+|\xi|^{2})^{s'} |\hat u|^{2}$.  Mathlib gap: weighted
$L^{2}$ tail estimate;
\code{MeasureTheory.tendsto\_setIntegral\_compl} on a
$\sigma$-finite measure is the intended target.
\uses{lem:rk-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain C: Doyle--Moran PL approximation in dim 2}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C.1: reduce to the disk Schoenflies theorem]
\label{lem:rado-dim2-r1-disk-schoenflies}
\uses{lem:rado-dim2-r2-schoenflies-jordan}
Uniform PL approximation of an arbitrary planar homeomorphism
on a compact set reduces (via local charts and a finite open
cover) to the case of a homeomorphism of the closed disk to a
Jordan domain --- i.e.\ to Schoenflies' theorem on disks.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C.2: Schoenflies on disks via Jordan-curve arc-length parameterization]
\label{lem:rado-dim2-r2-schoenflies-jordan}
\uses{lem:rado-dim2-r3-jordan-curve-theorem}
A homeomorphism $\overline{D} \to \Omega$ to a Jordan domain
extends from a PL approximation of the boundary Jordan curve to
a PL approximation of the whole disk.  This depends on the
Jordan curve theorem to identify $\Omega$ as the bounded
component.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C.3: Jordan curve theorem in $\R^{2}$]
\label{lem:rado-dim2-r3-jordan-curve-theorem}
\uses{lem:rado-dim2-r4-winding-degree}
Every simple closed curve in $\R^{2}$ separates the plane into
exactly two connected components, exactly one of which is
bounded.  Proof via degree theory: for $p$ off the curve, the
winding number is well-defined and takes values $0$ or $\pm 1$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C.4: winding number via degree on $S^{1}$]
\label{lem:rado-dim2-r4-winding-degree}
\uses{lem:rado-dim2-r5-pi1-circle}
For a continuous loop $\gamma : S^{1} \to \C \setminus \{p\}$,
its winding number around $p$ equals the degree of the induced
map $S^{1} \to S^{1}$ obtained by normalizing
$(\gamma(t)-p)/|\gamma(t)-p|$.
\lean{Complex.windingNumber}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C.5: $\pi_{1}(S^{1}) = \Z$]
On the circle $S^{1}$, the fundamental group is $\Z$, with
generator the identity loop.  Mathlib status: present;
\code{Topology.Homotopy.Fundamental.Circle} provides
\code{fundamentalGroup\_circle\_iso\_int}.  This is the
Mathlib endpoint of the chain.
\label{lem:rado-dim2-r5-pi1-circle}
\lean{Topology.Homotopy.fundamentalGroup_circle_iso_int}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain D: Sum of residues equals zero}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D.1: Stokes on $X$ minus disks around poles]
\label{lem:srt-r1-stokes-cut-disks}
\uses{lem:srt-r2-residue-as-boundary}
Let $\omega$ be a meromorphic 1-form on a compact Riemann
surface $X$ with poles at $\{p_{i}\}$.  Cutting out small
disks $D_{i}$ around each pole and applying Stokes to
$d\omega = 0$ on $X \setminus \bigsqcup \overline{D_{i}}$ gives
$\sum_{i} \oint_{\partial D_{i}} \omega = 0$ (note the reversed
orientation of $\partial D_{i}$ as a boundary of $X \setminus
\bigsqcup D_{i}$).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D.2: each boundary integral is $2\pi i \cdot \mathrm{Res}_{p_{i}}\omega$]
\label{lem:srt-r2-residue-as-boundary}
\uses{lem:srt-r3-cauchy-formula-disk}
For a meromorphic 1-form $\omega$ on a chart-disk $D \ni p$
with the pole at $p$ as its only singularity,
$\oint_{\partial D} \omega = 2\pi i \cdot \mathrm{Res}_{p}\omega$.
This is a chart-local Cauchy residue identity.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D.3: Cauchy integral formula on a chart disk]
\label{lem:srt-r3-cauchy-formula-disk}
\uses{lem:srt-r4-dbar-delta}
For $f$ holomorphic on a punctured disk $D \setminus \{0\}$
with a pole at $0$ and Laurent expansion
$f = \sum_{n \geq -k} a_{n} z^{n}$,
$\oint_{\partial D} f(z)\,dz = 2\pi i\,a_{-1}$.  Equivalently,
$f(z)\,dz - a_{-1}\,d\log z$ is exact on $D \setminus \{0\}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D.4: distributional $\bar\partial(1/z) = \pi \delta_{0}$]
\label{lem:srt-r4-dbar-delta}
\uses{lem:srt-r5-pompeiu-link}
In the sense of distributions on $\C$, $\bar\partial (1/(\pi z))
= \delta_{0}$.  This is the elementary fundamental solution
identity for $\bar\partial$ in one variable.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D.5: distributional identity from Cauchy--Pompeiu]
\label{lem:srt-r5-pompeiu-link}
\uses{lem:dpp-r3-stokes-on-disk}
Pairing $\bar\partial(1/(\pi z))$ against a test function
$\varphi \in C^{\infty}_{c}(\C)$ via integration by parts gives
$\langle \bar\partial(1/(\pi z)), \varphi\rangle =
-\frac{1}{\pi}\iint \frac{\bar\partial \varphi}{z}\,dA$,
which equals $\varphi(0)$ by the Cauchy--Pompeiu formula
(Lemma~\ref{lem:dpp-r3-stokes-on-disk}).  This is the
Mathlib endpoint and the place where chain~D fuses with
chain~A.
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain E: Existence of a good cover}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E.1: cover by geodesically convex normal balls]
\label{lem:gce-r1-convex-normal-cover}
\uses{lem:gce-r2-convex-intersection,lem:gce-r3-convex-diffeo-ball}
Pick a Riemannian metric $g$ on $M$ and, at each point $p$, a
normal-coordinate ball $B_{p}$ of radius less than the
\emph{convexity radius}.  Each $B_{p}$ is geodesically convex.
Refining to a locally finite subcover gives a cover $\{U_{i}\}$
where every nonempty finite intersection $U_{i_{1}} \cap \cdots
\cap U_{i_{k}}$ is geodesically convex (Lemma~\ref{lem:gce-r2-convex-intersection})
hence contractible (Lemma~\ref{lem:gce-r3-convex-diffeo-ball}).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E.2: finite intersections of geodesically convex sets are convex]
\label{lem:gce-r2-convex-intersection}
\uses{lem:gce-r4-riemannian-metric-exists}
If $U, V \subseteq M$ are geodesically convex, so is $U \cap V$:
for $p, q \in U \cap V$, the unique minimizing geodesic from $p$
to $q$ lies in both $U$ and $V$.
\lean{GeodesicallyConvex}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E.3: a geodesically convex set is diffeomorphic to a ball]
\label{lem:gce-r3-convex-diffeo-ball}
\uses{lem:gce-r4-riemannian-metric-exists}
The exponential map $\exp_{p}$ at any interior point $p$ of a
geodesically convex set $U$ is a diffeomorphism from
$\exp_{p}^{-1}(U) \subseteq T_{p}M$ (which is a star-shaped
open subset of a vector space) onto $U$.  Hence $U$ is
contractible.
\lean{Manifold.exp}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E.4: every smooth manifold admits a Riemannian metric]
\label{lem:gce-r4-riemannian-metric-exists}
\uses{lem:gce-r5-smooth-pou}
Glue chart-local Euclidean inner products on the tangent bundle
via a smooth partition of unity to obtain a positive-definite
section of $\mathrm{Sym}^{2} T^{*} M$.
\lean{RiemannianMetric}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E.5: smooth partitions of unity exist on a paracompact smooth manifold]
\label{lem:gce-r5-smooth-pou}
Every locally finite open cover of a paracompact smooth
manifold admits a subordinate smooth partition of unity.
Mathlib status: present;
\code{SmoothManifoldWithCorners.SmoothPartitionOfUnity.exists\_isSubordinate}
is the named declaration.  This is the Mathlib endpoint.
\lean{SmoothManifoldWithCorners.SmoothPartitionOfUnity.exists_isSubordinate}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{layman}
This subsection makes the gap-to-Mathlib quantitative: each of
five frontier leaves has been refined five steps deeper, and at
the bottom of each chain the very last sub-statement is a named
Mathlib declaration (or one chart-localization away from one).
A leaf at depth-5 means a single Aristotle-style sub-project of
roughly 200--500 lines of Lean once the chain is unfolded into
forward declarations.  Most chains converge on the same handful
of Mathlib hooks: smooth partitions of unity, polar
change-of-variables, Fourier transform of tempered
distributions, fundamental group of $S^{1}$.  These four
endpoints are where the entire frontier of the project meets
ordinary Mathlib.
\end{layman}

\subsection{Frontier gap analysis: extended 5-pass refinements}
\label{subsec:frontier-five-pass-extended}

This subsection extends the 5-pass refinement methodology to the
remaining 48 leaves of the dependency graph.  The same pattern
applies: each chain refines a leaf five levels deep, and pass~5
either lands on a named Mathlib declaration or explicitly flags
the gap as ABSENT (no Mathlib hook yet) / partial (some pieces
exist).  The chains are organized by area (sections of this
document or sub-gaps R1--R10).

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain F1: Holomorphic $k$-th root (lem:analytic-kth-root)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1.1: reduce to a holomorphic logarithm]
\label{lem:akr-r1}
\uses{lem:akr-r2}
$h := \exp((1/k)\log g)$ produces the desired root once $\log g$ is well-defined on a small disk avoiding $0$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1.2: logarithm exists on a simply-connected nbhd avoiding $0$]
\label{lem:akr-r2}
\uses{lem:akr-r3}
On any simply-connected open $U \subseteq \C \setminus \{0\}$, $\log : U \to \C$ is holomorphic.  Reduces to existence of a primitive of $1/z$.
\lean{Complex.analyticAt_log}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1.3: primitive on simply-connected domain]
\label{lem:akr-r3}
\uses{lem:akr-r4}
On a simply-connected open in $\C$, every holomorphic function has a holomorphic primitive (Cauchy's theorem on simply-connected domains).
\lean{Mathlib.Analysis.Complex.CauchyIntegral}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1.4: Cauchy's theorem on a simply-connected domain]
\label{lem:akr-r4}
\uses{lem:akr-r5}
For $f$ holomorphic on a simply-connected open $U$, every closed loop in $U$ has $\oint f\,dz = 0$.  Reduces by Stokes to $df = 0$ holomorphic $\Rightarrow$ closed.
\lean{Mathlib.Analysis.Complex.CauchyIntegral}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1.5: Mathlib endpoint: $\log$ on a simply-connected nbhd avoiding $0$]
\label{lem:akr-r5}
\code{Complex.analyticAt\_log} provides $\log$ analytic at any point of $\C \setminus \{0\}$; combined with simply-connectedness this gives the global $\log$.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{Complex.analyticAt_log}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain F2: Properness of fibre below an open neighborhood (lem:proper-fiber-shrink)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2.1: complement of $U$ is compact]
\label{lem:pfs-r1}
\uses{lem:pfs-r2}
$U^{c}$ is closed in the compact source, hence compact.
\lean{IsClosed.isCompact}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2.2: image of a compact set is compact]
\label{lem:pfs-r2}
\uses{lem:pfs-r3}
$f(U^{c})$ is compact in the Hausdorff target $Y$.
\lean{IsCompact.image}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2.3: compact subsets of Hausdorff are closed]
\label{lem:pfs-r3}
\uses{lem:pfs-r4}
$f(U^{c})$ is closed in $Y$.
\lean{IsCompact.isClosed}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2.4: $y_{0}$ avoids the closed set $f(U^{c})$]
\label{lem:pfs-r4}
\uses{lem:pfs-r5}
By hypothesis $f^{-1}(y_{0}) \subseteq U$, so $y_{0} \notin f(U^{c})$.  The complement $V := Y \setminus f(U^{c})$ is open and contains $y_{0}$; for $y \in V$, $f^{-1}(y) \subseteq U$.
\lean{Set.compl}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2.5: Mathlib endpoint: closed-image-of-compact under continuous-Hausdorff]
\label{lem:pfs-r5}
\code{IsCompact.image} and \code{IsCompact.isClosed} (the latter on T2 spaces) compose to the needed fact.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{IsCompact.image_of_continuousOn}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain F7: Complex-torus package: $V/\Lambda$ is a compact complex Lie group (prop:complex-torus-package)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F7.1: quotient inherits AddCommGroup]
\label{lem:ctp-r1}
\uses{lem:ctp-r2}
$V/\Lambda$ is the quotient of an abelian group by a subgroup, so the quotient is an abelian group via the universal property.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F7.2: quotient inherits topology]
\label{lem:ctp-r2}
\uses{lem:ctp-r3}
Equip $V/\Lambda$ with the quotient topology of the projection $\pi : V \to V/\Lambda$.  $\pi$ is open, so this gives a topological group structure.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F7.3: quotient is Hausdorff and compact]
\label{lem:ctp-r3}
\uses{lem:ctp-r4}
$\Lambda$ closed and discrete in $V \cong \C^{g}$, so $V/\Lambda$ is Hausdorff.  Choose a fundamental parallelepiped $P$ for $\Lambda$; $\pi$ is surjective $P \to V/\Lambda$ with $P$ compact, hence the quotient is compact.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F7.4: complex chart at every point]
\label{lem:ctp-r4}
\uses{lem:ctp-r5}
Locally $\pi$ is a homeomorphism (since $\Lambda$ is discrete), so it pulls back a complex chart from $V$.  Charts at different points are related by translations, which are biholomorphic.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F7.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:ctp-r5}
\code{QuotientAddGroup.instTopologicalGroup} + \code{ChartedSpace.instOfHomeomorph} + \code{ComplexManifold} smooth-structure transfer.  Mathlib status: partial (charted-space transfer along an open quotient is the missing piece).
\uses{lem:ctp-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain F8: Pullback of holomorphic 1-forms along a holomorphic map (lem:pullback-forms)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F8.1: define $f^{*}\omega$ chart-locally]
\label{lem:pbf-r1}
\uses{lem:pbf-r2}
In holomorphic charts $w$ on $X$ and $z$ on $Y$, write $\omega = g(z)\,dz$ and $f$ as $z = F(w)$ holomorphic.  Define $f^{*}\omega := g(F(w))\,F'(w)\,dw$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F8.2: chart-independence of the pullback]
\label{lem:pbf-r2}
\uses{lem:pbf-r3}
Under change of holomorphic chart $w' = \varphi(w)$, the chain rule gives $f^{*}\omega$ the correct transformation, so the chart-local formula glues to a global section of $f^{*}\Omega^{1}_{Y}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F8.3: naturality on overlaps reduces to the chain rule]
\label{lem:pbf-r3}
\uses{lem:pbf-r4}
On chart overlaps, the pullback transforms via $(F \circ \varphi)' = F'(\varphi)\cdot\varphi'$.  Same on the other side via the cocycle condition for $\Omega^{1}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F8.4: chain rule for holomorphic functions]
\label{lem:pbf-r4}
\uses{lem:pbf-r5}
$(g \circ F)'(w) = g'(F(w)) \cdot F'(w)$ for holomorphic $g, F$, by the complex differentiability.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F8.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:pbf-r5}
\code{HasDerivAt.comp} and \code{ContMDiff.compMDifferentiable} provide the chart-local + manifold-level chain rules.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{HasDerivAt.comp}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain F9: Trace of a holomorphic 1-form along a finite branched cover (lem:trace-forms)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F9.1: define $\mathrm{tr}\,\omega$ on the unbranched locus]
\label{lem:trf-r1}
\uses{lem:trf-r2}
Off the branch locus, $f : X \to Y$ is an unramified cover of degree $d$.  Define $(\mathrm{tr}\,\omega)(y) := \sum_{x \in f^{-1}(y)} \omega_{x}$ in pulled-back local coordinates.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F9.2: trace extends across the branch locus]
\label{lem:trf-r2}
\uses{lem:trf-r3}
Near a ramified point with index $k$, the $k$ sheets coalesce; the sum of $\omega$ over the fiber extends continuously (and holomorphically) to a 1-form on $Y$ via the $k$-th power expansion.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F9.3: local trace formula in branched coordinates]
\label{lem:trf-r3}
\uses{lem:trf-r4}
In coordinates $w = z^{k}$ near a branch point, $\sum_{j=0}^{k-1} f(\zeta_{k}^{j} z)\,d(\zeta_{k}^{j} z) = k \cdot f^{\#}(w)\,dw$ for $f^{\#}(w) := \frac{1}{k}\sum_{j} f(\zeta_{k}^{j} w^{1/k})$ holomorphic in $w$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F9.4: Newton symmetric-function theorem]
\label{lem:trf-r4}
\uses{lem:trf-r5}
Symmetric functions in $\zeta_{k}^{j} z^{1/k}$ that are smooth in the input are smooth in $w = z^{k}$.  Reduces to the Newton identities expressing power sums in elementary symmetric polynomials.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F9.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:trf-r5}
\code{MvPolynomial.symmetric\_subalgebra} is Mathlib-present in the polynomial setting; the analytic upgrade for power series is the named declaration we need.  Mathlib status: partial.
\uses{lem:trf-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B1: Finite disk atlas exists (compactness) (lem:rado-finite-disk-atlas)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B1.1: cover by chart neighborhoods]
\label{lem:rfd-r1}
\uses{lem:rfd-r2}
Every point of $X$ has a chart $\varphi_{p} : U_{p} \to \R^{2}$.  Restricting to $V_{p} \subset U_{p}$ with $\overline{V_{p}}$ compactly contained gives an open cover by relatively compact disk-like sets.
\lean{ChartedSpace.atlas}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B1.2: compactness extracts a finite subcover]
\label{lem:rfd-r2}
\uses{lem:rfd-r3}
$X$ compact, so $\{V_{p}\}$ admits a finite subcover $\{V_{1}, \ldots, V_{N}\}$.
\lean{IsCompact.elim_finite_subcover}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B1.3: disk shapes preserved on overlaps]
\label{lem:rfd-r3}
\uses{lem:rfd-r4}
Pre-images $\varphi_{i}(V_{i}) \subseteq \R^{2}$ are smooth disks; overlap regions are smooth-diffeomorphic via the transition maps $\varphi_{j}\circ\varphi_{i}^{-1}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B1.4: paracompactness of compact 2-manifolds]
\label{lem:rfd-r4}
\uses{lem:rfd-r5}
Every compact $C^{0}$-manifold is paracompact; we just used compactness, but the subordinate finite cover argument works under weaker paracompactness.
\lean{TopologicalSpace.IsParacompact}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B1.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:rfd-r5}
\code{IsCompact.elim\_finite\_subcover} and \code{ChartedSpace.atlas} are the Mathlib hooks.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{IsCompact.elim_finite_subcover}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B2: PL cocycle preservation under chart modification (lem:rado-pl-cocycle-preservation)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B2.1: PL transition functions form a cocycle]
\label{lem:rpc-r1}
\uses{lem:rpc-r2}
On overlaps $U_{i}\cap U_{j}\cap U_{k}$ the transition maps $\varphi_{ij}, \varphi_{jk}, \varphi_{ki}$ satisfy $\varphi_{ki}\circ\varphi_{jk}\circ\varphi_{ij} = \mathrm{id}$.  Modifying $\varphi_{i}$ by a PL homeomorphism $\psi$ changes only $\varphi_{ij}$ to $\varphi_{ij}\circ\psi^{-1}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B2.2: composition with a PL map preserves PL]
\label{lem:rpc-r2}
\uses{lem:rpc-r3}
PL maps are closed under composition: $f, g$ both piecewise linear $\Rightarrow$ $f\circ g$ piecewise linear with finitely many regions.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B2.3: PL composition refines the simplicial decomposition]
\label{lem:rpc-r3}
\uses{lem:rpc-r4}
$f\circ g$ admits a common simplicial subdivision: take the simplicial preimage of $g$'s decomposition under $f^{-1}$, then refine.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B2.4: simplicial complex refinement existence]
\label{lem:rpc-r4}
\uses{lem:rpc-r5}
Any two finite simplicial decompositions of a region in $\R^{2}$ admit a common refinement (e.g.\ via the union of all simplex boundaries).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B2.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:rpc-r5}
PL infrastructure is currently absent from Mathlib.  Closest hooks: \code{Mathlib.Analysis.Convex.Simplex} for simplices and \code{AffineMap} for affine maps.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:rpc-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B3: Triangulate each chart (lem:rado-triangulate-each-chart)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B3.1: standard triangulation of a planar disk]
\label{lem:rtc-r1}
\uses{lem:rtc-r2}
The standard closed disk $\overline{B^{2}}$ admits an explicit triangulation, e.g.\ by radial wedges from the center plus circular arcs.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B3.2: transport triangulation through chart homeomorphism]
\label{lem:rtc-r2}
\uses{lem:rtc-r3}
For chart $\varphi : U \to D$, pull back the standard triangulation via $\varphi^{-1}$; this gives a topological triangulation of $U$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B3.3: piecewise-linearity of the transported triangulation]
\label{lem:rtc-r3}
\uses{lem:rtc-r4}
After applying the local PL approximation (Doyle--Moran), the transported triangulation has PL transitions between charts.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B3.4: subdivision to match overlap data]
\label{lem:rtc-r4}
\uses{lem:rtc-r5}
Refine each chart's triangulation finely enough that overlaps land within sub-simplices, enabling chart-by-chart gluing.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B3.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:rtc-r5}
\code{Topology.Homotopy.Triangulation} is the natural target; the relevant simplicial-complex API is currently a project-side fork.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:rtc-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B4: Common subdivision over overlaps (lem:rado-common-subdivision)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B4.1: two triangulations of the same disk admit a common refinement]
\label{lem:rcs-r1}
\uses{lem:rcs-r2}
Any two triangulations $\mathcal{T}_{1}, \mathcal{T}_{2}$ of a closed disk admit a common subdivision $\mathcal{T}$, refining both.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B4.2: planar arrangement of edges]
\label{lem:rcs-r2}
\uses{lem:rcs-r3}
Take the union of all 1-cells of $\mathcal{T}_{1}$ and $\mathcal{T}_{2}$ as a planar 1-complex.  Each face of the resulting arrangement is a polygon, triangulable in finitely many ways.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B4.3: polygon triangulation by ear-cutting]
\label{lem:rcs-r3}
\uses{lem:rcs-r4}
Every simple polygon admits a triangulation, e.g.\ by repeatedly cutting an `ear' (a triangle whose third edge is internal).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B4.4: ear-existence theorem for simple polygons]
\label{lem:rcs-r4}
\uses{lem:rcs-r5}
Every simple polygon with $\geq 4$ vertices has at least two ears.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B4.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:rcs-r5}
Polygon ear-theorem is computational geometry; not in current Mathlib.  Closest: \code{Polygon} basic API in Mathlib3 not yet ported.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:rcs-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B5: Edge condition: each 1-simplex in two 2-simplices (lem:rado-edges-in-two-triangles)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B5.1: local 2-manifold structure $\Rightarrow$ each edge has two sides]
\label{lem:ret-r1}
\uses{lem:ret-r2}
At any interior edge $e$ of a triangulation of a 2-manifold, a small neighborhood of $e$ is homeomorphic to $\R^{2}$, hence has exactly two sides.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B5.2: each side is a triangle]
\label{lem:ret-r2}
\uses{lem:ret-r3}
In a finite triangulation, the only 2-cells incident to $e$ are triangles, and the local 2-manifold condition forces exactly one triangle on each side.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B5.3: non-boundary edges of a closed manifold lie in exactly two triangles]
\label{lem:ret-r3}
\uses{lem:ret-r4}
For a closed (boundaryless) 2-manifold, every edge is interior and so every edge is in exactly two triangles.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B5.4: local Euclidean structure gives a 2-disk neighborhood of an edge]
\label{lem:ret-r4}
\uses{lem:ret-r5}
By the manifold chart at a midpoint of $e$, a neighborhood is homeomorphic to $\R^{2}$, in which an edge separates the plane into two pieces (Jordan-arc theorem).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B5.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:ret-r5}
\code{Topology.JordanCurve} (planned) for the arc-separation step.  Mathlib status: ABSENT (Jordan arc theorem in Mathlib).
\uses{lem:ret-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B6: Vertex link is a combinatorial circle (lem:rado-vertex-link-is-circle)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B6.1: local structure at a vertex of a 2-manifold]
\label{lem:rvl-r1}
\uses{lem:rvl-r2}
A small neighborhood of a vertex $v$ is homeomorphic to $\R^{2}$; the link of $v$ in the triangulation corresponds to the boundary of a small disk around $v$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B6.2: ordering edges around a vertex]
\label{lem:rvl-r2}
\uses{lem:rvl-r3}
The triangles at $v$ can be cyclically ordered by their edges incident to $v$, since each pair of adjacent triangles shares an edge.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B6.3: link is a 1-cycle in the triangulation]
\label{lem:rvl-r3}
\uses{lem:rvl-r4}
Cyclically traversing the triangles at $v$ produces a closed 1-cycle in the link, i.e.\ a combinatorial circle.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B6.4: non-branching of the link follows from the edge condition]
\label{lem:rvl-r4}
\uses{lem:rvl-r5}
By the edge condition (two triangles per edge), no vertex of the link has degree $\neq 2$, so the link is a 1-manifold without boundary in dimension 1, i.e.\ a finite disjoint union of circles, and connectedness gives one circle.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B6.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:rvl-r5}
PL link computation; not yet in Mathlib.  Closest: \code{Polynomial.Cyclic} for cyclic structures.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:rvl-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B7: Glued chart map is continuous (lem:rado-glue-continuous)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B7.1: chart maps are continuous on each chart]
\label{lem:rgc-r1}
\uses{lem:rgc-r2}
By definition a chart $\varphi_{i} : U_{i} \to \R^{2}$ is a homeomorphism, so continuous.
\lean{LocalHomeomorph.continuous}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B7.2: compatibility on overlaps]
\label{lem:rgc-r2}
\uses{lem:rgc-r3}
On $U_{i}\cap U_{j}$, $\varphi_{i}$ and $\varphi_{j}$ are related by a PL transition $\varphi_{ji}\circ\varphi_{i}$, which is continuous.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B7.3: gluing continuous maps over an open cover]
\label{lem:rgc-r3}
\uses{lem:rgc-r4}
A function $f : X \to Y$ defined on a finite open cover $\{U_{i}\}$ via continuous local pieces $f_{i}$, agreeing on overlaps, is continuous.
\lean{continuous_iff_continuousOn_iUnion_of_isOpen}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B7.4: pasting lemma for continuity]
\label{lem:rgc-r4}
\uses{lem:rgc-r5}
For an open cover $\{U_{i}\}$ of $X$, $f$ is continuous iff each $f|_{U_{i}}$ is continuous.
\lean{continuous_iff_continuousOn_iUnion_of_isOpen}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B7.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:rgc-r5}
\code{continuous\_iff\_continuousOn\_iUnion\_of\_isOpen} is Mathlib's pasting lemma.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{continuous_iff_continuousOn_iUnion_of_isOpen}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain B8: Glued chart map is bijective (lem:rado-glue-bijective)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B8.1: local injectivity on each chart]
\label{lem:rgb-r1}
\uses{lem:rgb-r2}
Each chart $\varphi_{i}$ is a homeomorphism, hence injective.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B8.2: agreement on overlaps lifts to global injectivity]
\label{lem:rgb-r2}
\uses{lem:rgb-r3}
If two points $p \neq q$ map to the same image, they lie in different charts (else local injectivity is contradicted); the chart-overlap compatibility produces a contradiction.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B8.3: surjectivity from cover-by-image]
\label{lem:rgb-r3}
\uses{lem:rgb-r4}
Every point of the target lies in $\varphi_{i}(U_{i})$ for some $i$, since the source $X$ is covered by $\{U_{i}\}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B8.4: disjoint-overlap analysis]
\label{lem:rgb-r4}
\uses{lem:rgb-r5}
On overlaps the glued map agrees by construction, so the map is well-defined as a quotient of the disjoint union of charts modulo the cocycle relation.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass B8.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:rgb-r5}
\code{Equiv.coe\_iUnion} for piecing together bijections from disjoint pieces; the cover-with-overlap variant requires a coproduct argument.  Mathlib status: partial.
\uses{lem:rgb-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain C1: Cyclic reduction to fully-reduced form (lem:tietze-cyclic-reduction)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C1.1: identify cancellable adjacent pairs]
\label{lem:tcr-r1}
\uses{lem:tcr-r2}
In an edge word $w = a_{1}\cdots a_{n}$ over $\{a, a^{-1}, b, b^{-1}, \ldots\}$, cancellable pairs are adjacent letters $a_{i} a_{i+1}$ with $a_{i+1} = a_{i}^{-1}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C1.2: cancel iteratively]
\label{lem:tcr-r2}
\uses{lem:tcr-r3}
While a cancellable pair exists, remove it.  Process terminates because word length strictly decreases.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C1.3: cyclic cancellability]
\label{lem:tcr-r3}
\uses{lem:tcr-r4}
Also cancel $a_{1}$ and $a_{n}$ if they are inverses (cyclic adjacency).  The cyclically-reduced form is unique up to rotation.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C1.4: uniqueness of reduced form (Stallings--Tietze)]
\label{lem:tcr-r4}
\uses{lem:tcr-r5}
Free groups have unique reduced words; cyclic reduction extends this uniqueness up to rotation.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C1.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:tcr-r5}
\code{FreeGroup} has \code{FreeGroup.reduce} for word reduction.  Mathlib status: present (free group reduce); cyclic version is partial.
\uses{lem:tcr-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain C2: Brahana handle creation (lem:tietze-brahana)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C2.1: identify a `crossing' pattern in the word]
\label{lem:tbh-r1}
\uses{lem:tbh-r2}
In a cyclically-reduced edge word, a `crossing' is a pattern $a \cdots b \cdots a^{-1} \cdots b^{-1}$ where $a$ and $b$ alternate.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C2.2: re-identify crossings as a handle]
\label{lem:tbh-r2}
\uses{lem:tbh-r3}
Each crossing can be rewritten as a handle block $aba^{-1}b^{-1}$ via a sequence of Tietze moves preserving the underlying surface.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C2.3: Tietze moves preserve homotopy type]
\label{lem:tbh-r3}
\uses{lem:tbh-r4}
Tietze moves correspond to choices of base-point or path; they don't change the surface up to homeomorphism.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C2.4: group-theoretic version of handle creation]
\label{lem:tbh-r4}
\uses{lem:tbh-r5}
In the free group on $\{a, b, c, d, \ldots\}$, the relator can be transformed via conjugation and substitution to expose handle blocks.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C2.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:tbh-r5}
\code{PresentedGroup.relations\_eq} for relator manipulation.  Brahana handle creation specifically not in Mathlib.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:tbh-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain C3: Tietze moves preserve orientability (lem:tietze-moves-preserve-orientability)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C3.1: orientability via consistent edge labeling]
\label{lem:tmo-r1}
\uses{lem:tmo-r2}
A polygon edge word represents an orientable surface iff each letter and its inverse appear with consistent signs (no $aa$ pattern).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C3.2: Tietze moves do not introduce same-orientation pairs]
\label{lem:tmo-r2}
\uses{lem:tmo-r3}
The basic Tietze moves (relator manipulation, generator addition/removal) preserve the consistent-sign property.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C3.3: inductive proof on move count]
\label{lem:tmo-r3}
\uses{lem:tmo-r4}
By induction on the number of Tietze moves, the orientability invariant propagates.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C3.4: equivalence of polygon-word orientability and surface orientability]
\label{lem:tmo-r4}
\uses{lem:tmo-r5}
The combinatorial orientability of the polygon word matches the topological orientability of the resulting surface.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C3.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:tmo-r5}
Surface orientability is currently absent from Mathlib.  Closest: \code{Manifold.Orientable} (planned).  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:tmo-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain C4: Orientable handle-grouped is concatenation of standard blocks (lem:tietze-orientable-is-handle-concat)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C4.1: structure theorem for handle-grouped words]
\label{lem:toh-r1}
\uses{lem:toh-r2}
A handle-grouped word has the form $\prod_{i=1}^{g} W_{i}$ where each $W_{i}$ is a 4-letter handle block.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C4.2: each handle block is in standard form $a_{i}b_{i}a_{i}^{-1}b_{i}^{-1}$]
\label{lem:toh-r2}
\uses{lem:toh-r3}
By renaming generators within each block, $W_{i}$ takes the standard form.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C4.3: concatenation preserves orientability]
\label{lem:toh-r3}
\uses{lem:toh-r4}
The product structure is preserved under the standard surface concatenation operation.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C4.4: uniqueness of the standard form up to genus]
\label{lem:toh-r4}
\uses{lem:toh-r5}
Two orientable surface words are equivalent iff their handle counts agree.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C4.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:toh-r5}
Genus classification of orientable surfaces is currently absent from Mathlib.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:toh-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain C5: Handle blocks may be permuted within a word (lem:tietze-handle-swap)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C5.1: two handles commute up to Tietze moves]
\label{lem:ths-r1}
\uses{lem:ths-r2}
Two adjacent handle blocks $H_{i}H_{i+1}$ in a word can be swapped to $H_{i+1}H_{i}$ via a sequence of Tietze moves.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C5.2: explicit swap via auxiliary moves]
\label{lem:ths-r2}
\uses{lem:ths-r3}
Use cyclic permutation + relator conjugation to move one block past another, preserving the underlying group presentation.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C5.3: conjugation in free product groups]
\label{lem:ths-r3}
\uses{lem:ths-r4}
In the free group, conjugating one factor by another doesn't change the underlying group, only the relator presentation.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C5.4: permutations generated by adjacent transpositions]
\label{lem:ths-r4}
\uses{lem:ths-r5}
Any permutation of $\{1, \ldots, g\}$ is a product of adjacent transpositions, so handle ordering is fully arbitrary.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C5.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:ths-r5}
\code{Equiv.Perm} for permutations; conjugation in \code{FreeGroup} is partial.  Mathlib status: partial.
\uses{lem:ths-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain C6: Strip-one-handle (induction) (lem:tietze-strip-one-handle)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C6.1: isolate the first handle block]
\label{lem:tsh-r1}
\uses{lem:tsh-r2}
Use cyclic reduction + handle swap to isolate one handle block at the start of the word.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C6.2: strip the handle via free-product factorization]
\label{lem:tsh-r2}
\uses{lem:tsh-r3}
The word decomposes as $W = H \cdot W'$ where $H$ is the first handle block, $W'$ has $g-1$ handles.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C6.3: inductive hypothesis on $W'$]
\label{lem:tsh-r3}
\uses{lem:tsh-r4}
By induction on $g$, $W'$ is a concatenation of $g-1$ standard handles.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C6.4: re-attach $H$ to obtain the full standard form]
\label{lem:tsh-r4}
\uses{lem:tsh-r5}
Concatenating $H$ with the inductively-derived form gives the full standard relator $\prod_{i=1}^{g} a_{i}b_{i}a_{i}^{-1}b_{i}^{-1}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass C6.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:tsh-r5}
Inductive proof using \code{Nat.rec}; relator decomposition is not in Mathlib.  Mathlib status: partial (only the induction primitive is present).
\uses{lem:tsh-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain D1: Cut along a spanning tree of the 1-skeleton (lem:polygonal-model-cut-along-tree)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D1.1: spanning tree exists in any connected finite graph]
\label{lem:pct-r1}
\uses{lem:pct-r2}
Take a maximal acyclic subgraph of the 1-skeleton; this is a spanning tree.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D1.2: cutting along the tree produces a topological disk]
\label{lem:pct-r2}
\uses{lem:pct-r3}
Removing a spanning tree from a triangulated 2-manifold and taking the dual gives a topological disk.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D1.3: Euler-formula check on the disk]
\label{lem:pct-r3}
\uses{lem:pct-r4}
$V - E + F = 1$ for a disk; verifying this on the cut produces consistency.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D1.4: disk has length-$4g$ boundary edge word]
\label{lem:pct-r4}
\uses{lem:pct-r5}
For a genus-$g$ surface, the dual disk has $2 \cdot 2g$ boundary edges in a four-letter alphabet.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D1.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:pct-r5}
\code{SimpleGraph.spanningTree} (planned) for the spanning tree.  Cutting argument is not in Mathlib.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:pct-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Chain D2: Polygonal-model genus matches topological genus (lem:polygonal-model-genus-matches)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D2.1: Euler characteristic from triangulation]
\label{lem:pgm-r1}
\uses{lem:pgm-r2}
$\chi(X) = V - E + F$ for any triangulation.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D2.2: Euler characteristic of standard $4g$-gon model]
\label{lem:pgm-r2}
\uses{lem:pgm-r3}
The standard model identifies $4g$ edges in pairs, $V = 1$ vertex (all corners identified), $E = 2g$, $F = 1$, giving $\chi = 2 - 2g$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D2.3: topological genus from Euler characteristic]
\label{lem:pgm-r3}
\uses{lem:pgm-r4}
For a closed orientable surface, $\chi = 2 - 2g$ defines the topological genus uniquely.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D2.4: invariance of $\chi$ under Tietze moves]
\label{lem:pgm-r4}
\uses{lem:pgm-r5}
Each Tietze move preserves $\chi$: handle creation, swap, and reduction all preserve the count.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass D2.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:pgm-r5}
\code{EulerCharacteristic} for simplicial complexes is absent from Mathlib.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:pgm-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Chain E1: Integration is well-defined on smooth simplices (lem:deRham-integration-simplex)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E1.1: smooth simplex parametrization]
\label{lem:dis-r1}
\uses{lem:dis-r2}
A smooth $k$-simplex in $M$ is a smooth map $\sigma : \Delta^{k} \to M$ from the standard simplex.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E1.2: pullback of a $k$-form]
\label{lem:dis-r2}
\uses{lem:dis-r3}
$\sigma^{*}\omega$ is a smooth $k$-form on $\Delta^{k} \subseteq \R^{k}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E1.3: integration of a top form on $\Delta^{k}$]
\label{lem:dis-r3}
\uses{lem:dis-r4}
$\int_{\Delta^{k}} \sigma^{*}\omega$ is the standard Lebesgue integral of a smooth function on the compact simplex.
\lean{MeasureTheory.integral}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E1.4: convergence + finiteness from compactness]
\label{lem:dis-r4}
\uses{lem:dis-r5}
$\sigma^{*}\omega$ is continuous, hence bounded on compact $\Delta^{k}$; the integral is finite.
\lean{MeasureTheory.IntegrableOn}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E1.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:dis-r5}
\code{MeasureTheory.integral} on a compact set with continuous integrand.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{MeasureTheory.integral_continuous_of_compact}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain E2: Stokes for a simplex: $\int_{\sigma} d\omega = \int_{\partial\sigma} \omega$ (lem:deRham-stokes-simplex)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E2.1: reduce to the standard simplex]
\label{lem:dss-r1}
\uses{lem:dss-r2}
Pull back via $\sigma$: $\int_{\sigma}d\omega = \int_{\Delta^{k}}\sigma^{*}d\omega = \int_{\Delta^{k}}d(\sigma^{*}\omega)$ by naturality.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E2.2: Stokes on the standard simplex]
\label{lem:dss-r2}
\uses{lem:dss-r3}
$\int_{\Delta^{k}} d\eta = \int_{\partial\Delta^{k}}\eta$ for $\eta$ smooth, where $\partial\Delta^{k}$ is the alternating sum of faces.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E2.3: face decomposition of $\partial\Delta^{k}$]
\label{lem:dss-r3}
\uses{lem:dss-r4}
$\partial\Delta^{k} = \sum_{i=0}^{k}(-1)^{i}\partial_{i}$ where $\partial_{i}$ is the $i$-th face inclusion.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E2.4: Stokes on a half-space (FTC induction)]
\label{lem:dss-r4}
\uses{lem:dss-r5}
By induction on $k$, Stokes on $\Delta^{k}$ reduces to the FTC on the unit interval combined with Fubini on lower-dim slices.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E2.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:dss-r5}
\code{intervalIntegral.integral\_deriv\_eq\_sub} for FTC; \code{MeasureTheory.integral\_prod} for Fubini.  Stokes-on-simplex is partially in Mathlib via \code{Stokes\_theorem}.  Mathlib status: partial.
\uses{lem:dss-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain E3: Smooth-singular into singular is a quasi-iso (lem:deRham-smooth-quasi-iso)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E3.1: smooth-singular $\subseteq$ singular as sub-chain complex]
\label{lem:dsq-r1}
\uses{lem:dsq-r2}
Smooth simplices are continuous, so smooth-singular chains form a sub-chain complex of singular chains.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E3.2: Whitney smoothing: every continuous simplex is homotopic to a smooth one]
\label{lem:dsq-r2}
\uses{lem:dsq-r3}
For $\sigma : \Delta^{k} \to M$ continuous, there exists $\tilde\sigma$ smooth + a smooth homotopy $H : \Delta^{k}\times I \to M$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E3.3: homotopy gives chain-equivalence]
\label{lem:dsq-r3}
\uses{lem:dsq-r4}
The homotopy $H$ provides an explicit chain homotopy between $\sigma$ and $\tilde\sigma$, so the inclusion of complexes is a homology iso.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E3.4: Whitney approximation theorem on a smooth manifold]
\label{lem:dsq-r4}
\uses{lem:dsq-r5}
Continuous maps to a smooth manifold are dense in the compact-open topology, and any continuous map is homotopic to a smooth one.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E3.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:dsq-r5}
\code{Manifold.WhitneyApproximation} (planned).  Mathlib status: partial (basic smoothing in \code{ContDiff} is present, manifold-level approximation pending).
\uses{lem:dsq-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Chain E4: Mayer--Vietoris exact sequence (both theories) (lem:deRham-mayer-vietoris)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E4.1: MV for de Rham via short exact sequence of complexes]
\label{lem:dmv-r1}
\uses{lem:dmv-r2}
For an open cover $\{U, V\}$ of $X$, $0 \to \Omega^{*}(X) \to \Omega^{*}(U)\oplus\Omega^{*}(V) \to \Omega^{*}(U\cap V) \to 0$ is short-exact.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E4.2: MV for singular via barycentric subdivision]
\label{lem:dmv-r2}
\uses{lem:dmv-r3}
Singular MV is established via small simplices: every chain in $X$ is chain-homotopic to a small chain, decomposable into $U$- and $V$-pieces.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E4.3: snake lemma gives the long exact sequence]
\label{lem:dmv-r3}
\uses{lem:dmv-r4}
Both short exact sequences yield long exact sequences in cohomology via the snake lemma.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E4.4: comparison map is a chain map]
\label{lem:dmv-r4}
\uses{lem:dmv-r5}
The integration map $\Omega^{*} \to S^{*}$ commutes with the MV connecting morphism, giving a comparison of long exact sequences.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E4.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:dmv-r5}
\code{CategoryTheory.Abelian.SnakeLemma} for snake lemma; MV for singular cohomology partially present in \code{AlgebraicTopology}.  Mathlib status: partial.
\uses{lem:dmv-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Chain E5: Five-lemma induction over a finite good cover (lem:deRham-five-lemma-induction)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E5.1: base case: one chart]
\label{lem:dfl-r1}
\uses{lem:dfl-r2}
On a contractible chart, both de Rham and singular cohomology are trivial in positive degrees, so the integration map is trivially an iso.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E5.2: inductive step: combine two covers]
\label{lem:dfl-r2}
\uses{lem:dfl-r3}
Given covers of size $n$ and 1 covering $X$, combine via Mayer--Vietoris and the five lemma.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E5.3: five lemma for chain map between long exact sequences]
\label{lem:dfl-r3}
\uses{lem:dfl-r4}
If 4 of 5 maps in a ladder of long exact sequences are isos, so is the 5th.
\lean{CategoryTheory.Abelian.fiveLemma}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E5.4: induction on cover size]
\label{lem:dfl-r4}
\uses{lem:dfl-r5}
By induction on the size of a finite good cover, the comparison map becomes an iso globally.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass E5.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:dfl-r5}
\code{CategoryTheory.Abelian.FiveLemma}.  Mathlib status: present (in homological-algebra section).

\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Chain F1H: Laplacian is elliptic (lem:hodge-laplacian-elliptic)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1H.1: symbol of $\Delta$]
\label{lem:hle-r1}
\uses{lem:hle-r2}
In local coordinates, the principal symbol of $\Delta$ is $|\xi|^{2}$, a positive-definite quadratic form.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1H.2: ellipticity = invertible principal symbol]
\label{lem:hle-r2}
\uses{lem:hle-r3}
An operator is elliptic iff its principal symbol is invertible for every nonzero $\xi$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1H.3: $|\xi|^{2} > 0$ for $\xi \neq 0$]
\label{lem:hle-r3}
\uses{lem:hle-r4}
Positive-definiteness of the standard inner product on $T^{*}M$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1H.4: local representation of the Laplacian on $\R^{n}$]
\label{lem:hle-r4}
\uses{lem:hle-r5}
$\Delta = -\sum_{i,j} g^{ij}\partial_{i}\partial_{j} + \text{lower order}$.  The principal part is $-\partial_{i}\partial_{j}$, with symbol $\xi_{i}\xi_{j}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F1H.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:hle-r5}
\code{LinearPDE.Symbol} and \code{LinearPDE.Elliptic} are not in Mathlib.  Closest: \code{ContinuousLinearMap}.  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:hle-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain F2H: Hodge $L^{2}$-orthogonal decomposition (lem:hodge-orthogonal-decomposition)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2H.1: define harmonic forms as $\ker\Delta$]
\label{lem:hod-r1}
\uses{lem:hod-r2}
$\mathcal{H}^{k} := \{\omega \in \Omega^{k}(M) : \Delta\omega = 0\}$.
\lean{Mathlib.Analysis.NormedSpace.Banach.ClosedRange}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2H.2: $L^{2}$-decomposition $\Omega^{k} = \mathcal{H}^{k}\oplus\mathrm{im}\,d\oplus\mathrm{im}\,d^{*}$]
\label{lem:hod-r2}
\uses{lem:hod-r3}
The three subspaces are pairwise $L^{2}$-orthogonal: $\langle d\alpha, d^{*}\beta\rangle = \langle d^{2}\alpha, \beta\rangle = 0$, similarly for the others.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2H.3: density of $C^{\infty}$ in $L^{2}$]
\label{lem:hod-r3}
\uses{lem:hod-r4}
$C^{\infty}$ smooth forms are dense in $L^{2}$ via mollification + partition of unity.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2H.4: Fredholm + closed range theorem]
\label{lem:hod-r4}
\uses{lem:hod-r5}
$\Delta$ Fredholm $\Rightarrow$ closed range, so the orthogonal complement of $\ker\Delta$ is the image of $\Delta$, which equals $\mathrm{im}\,d \oplus \mathrm{im}\,d^{*}$.
\lean{InnerProductSpace.orthogonalProjection}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F2H.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:hod-r5}
\code{Banach.ClosedRange} for closed-range theorem; Hodge orthogonality is currently absent.  Mathlib status: partial.
\uses{lem:hod-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain F3H: Each de Rham class has a harmonic representative (lem:hodge-unique-harmonic-representative)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F3H.1: project a closed form onto $\mathcal{H}^{k}$]
\label{lem:huh-r1}
\uses{lem:huh-r2}
For $\omega \in \ker d$, decompose $\omega = h + d\alpha + d^{*}\beta$.  Closed-ness gives $d^{*}\beta = 0$, so $\omega = h + d\alpha$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F3H.2: uniqueness up to exact]
\label{lem:huh-r2}
\uses{lem:huh-r3}
Two harmonic representatives differing by an exact form must be equal: if $h_{1} - h_{2} = d\alpha$, taking inner product with $h_{1}-h_{2}$ gives $\|h_{1}-h_{2}\|^{2} = 0$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F3H.3: $d^{*}\beta = 0$ from $d\omega = 0$]
\label{lem:huh-r3}
\uses{lem:huh-r4}
$d\omega = 0$ and $\omega = h + d\alpha + d^{*}\beta$ give $d(d^{*}\beta) = 0$, then $\langle dd^{*}\beta, \beta\rangle = \|d^{*}\beta\|^{2} = 0$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F3H.4: Hodge inner product non-degeneracy]
\label{lem:huh-r4}
\uses{lem:huh-r5}
$\langle\cdot,\cdot\rangle_{L^{2}}$ is positive-definite on $\Omega^{k}(M)$ for compact $M$.
\lean{InnerProductSpace}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F3H.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:huh-r5}
\code{InnerProductSpace} for the inner product structure.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{InnerProductSpace.toEuclideanSpace}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\subsubsection*{Chain F4H: Kahler bigrading of harmonic forms (lem:hodge-bigrading-split)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F4H.1: Kahler identity $\Delta_{d} = 2\Delta_{\bar\partial}$]
\label{lem:hbs-r1}
\uses{lem:hbs-r2}
On a Kahler manifold, the de Rham Laplacian equals twice the Dolbeault Laplacian.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F4H.2: commutation of $\Delta$ with bidegree projections]
\label{lem:hbs-r2}
\uses{lem:hbs-r3}
$\Delta$ commutes with the bidegree projections $\Pi^{p,q}$: $\Delta\Pi^{p,q}\omega = \Pi^{p,q}\Delta\omega$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F4H.3: harmonic forms split by bidegree]
\label{lem:hbs-r3}
\uses{lem:hbs-r4}
$\mathcal{H}^{k} = \bigoplus_{p+q=k}\mathcal{H}^{p,q}$ where $\mathcal{H}^{p,q} := \mathcal{H}^{k}\cap\Omega^{p,q}$.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F4H.4: Riemann surface case: bidegrees $(1,0), (0,1)$]
\label{lem:hbs-r4}
\uses{lem:hbs-r5}
For a Riemann surface, $k=1$ has only $(p,q) \in \{(1,0),(0,1)\}$, giving $\mathcal{H}^{1} = \mathcal{H}^{1,0}\oplus\mathcal{H}^{0,1}$ with complex conjugation swapping them.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass F4H.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:hbs-r5}
\code{ComplexManifold.BidegreeDecomposition} (planned).  Mathlib status: ABSENT.
\uses{lem:hbs-r6}
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G1.1: $X$ is a finite CW complex]
\label{lem:hdf-r1}
\uses{lem:hdf-r2}
A compact 2-manifold has the homotopy type of a finite CW complex (Radó + Tietze).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\label{lem:hdf-r2}
\uses{lem:hdf-r3}
$H_{n}(X;\Z)$ is f.g.\ when $X$ is a finite CW complex (cellular chain complex is f.g.).
\notready
\end{lemma}

\label{lem:hdf-r3}
\uses{lem:hdf-r4}
For a CW complex, cellular and singular homology agree.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G1.4: cellular chain complex of a finite CW complex is f.g.]
\label{lem:hdf-r4}
\uses{lem:hdf-r5}
Each $C_{n}^{\mathrm{cell}}$ is a free $\Z$-module on a finite set of $n$-cells.
\notready
\end{lemma}

% --------------------------------------------------------------
\subsubsection*{Chain G2: Rank-Hom: $\dim_{\R}\mathrm{Hom}(A,\R) = \mathrm{rank}_{\Z} A$ for $A$ f.g. (lem:hodge-deRham-hom-rank)}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G2.1: decompose f.g.\ abelian $A = \Z^{r}\oplus T$ with $T$ torsion]
\label{lem:hdr-r1}
\uses{lem:hdr-r2}
Structure theorem: every f.g.\ abelian group is $\Z^{r}\oplus(\text{torsion})$.
\lean{AddCommGroup.Pi.AddCommGroupStructureTheorem}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G2.2: $\mathrm{Hom}(\Z, \R) = \R$]
\label{lem:hdr-r2}
\uses{lem:hdr-r3}
By evaluation at $1$, $\mathrm{Hom}(\Z,\R)$ is freely generated as $\R$-module by the identity, so it's $\R$.
\lean{Module.End.eval}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G2.3: $\mathrm{Hom}(T, \R) = 0$ for torsion $T$]
\label{lem:hdr-r3}
\uses{lem:hdr-r4}
For $\varphi \in \mathrm{Hom}_\Z(T, \R)$ and $a \in T$ of order $n \geq 1$, additivity gives $n\,\varphi(a) = \varphi(n\,a) = \varphi(0) = 0$ in $\R$; since $\R$ is torsion-free as a $\Z$-module ($n \neq 0$ implies multiplication by $n$ is injective on $\R$), $\varphi(a) = 0$.  Hence $\varphi$ vanishes on every torsion element, so $\varphi = 0$.  (No ``$\tfrac{1}{n}\varphi(na)$'' inversion is needed: division by $n$ in $\R$ witnesses the cancellation but is not a step in the proof.)
\lean{JacobianChallenge.Periods.homℤℝ_eq_zero_of_isTorsion}
\leanok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G2.4: $\mathrm{Hom}(\Z^{r}\oplus T,\R) = \R^{r}$]
\label{lem:hdr-r4}
\uses{lem:hdr-r5}
Combining: $\dim_{\R}\mathrm{Hom}(\Z^{r}\oplus T,\R) = r$.
\lean{Module.finrank}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G2.5: Mathlib endpoint]
\label{lem:hdr-r5}
\uses{lem:hdr-r6}
\code{Module.finrank} and \code{AddCommGroup.IsFiniteType}; structure theorem for f.g.\ abelian groups in \code{AddCommGroup.Pi.AddCommGroupStructureTheorem}.  Mathlib status: present.
\lean{Module.finrank_pi_fintype}
\leanok\mathlibok
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G3.1: UCT short exact sequence]

\uses{lem:hdu-r2}
$0 \to \mathrm{Ext}(H_{n-1}(X,\Z),\R) \to H^{n}(X,\R) \to \mathrm{Hom}(H_{n}(X,\Z),\R) \to 0$.
\notready

\label{lem:hdu-r2}
\uses{lem:hdu-r3}
$\R$ is divisible, hence injective as $\Z$-module, so $\mathrm{Ext}(\cdot,\R) = 0$ on any abelian group.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G3.3: $H^{n}(X,\R) \cong \mathrm{Hom}(H_{n}(X,\Z),\R)$]
\label{lem:hdu-r3}
\uses{lem:hdu-r4}
Combining UCT with vanishing Ext.
\notready
\end{lemma}

\begin{lemma}[Pass G3.4: specialize to $n=1$]
\label{lem:hdu-r4}
\uses{lem:hdu-r5}
$H^{1}(X,\R) \cong \mathrm{Hom}(H_{1}(X,\Z),\R)$.
\notready
\end{lemma}
